==PAGE:1==
Master Thesis project biomedical engineering
Automatic elimination of speech in audio
signals intended for sleep disorder
estimation using deep learning
Author: Malte Engmann Kjeldskov Jensen (s153962), Master’s student
Main supervisor: Helge B.D. Sørensen Associate Professor MSK, Ph.D.
Clinical Supervisor: Poul Jennum Professor, Chief Physician, MD, Ph.D.
Clinical Supervisor: Emmanuel Mignot Professor, Division Chief, MD, Ph.D.
February 16, 2020
==PAGE:2==
Preface
This thesis is a product of the Department of Biomedical Engineering at the Technical University of
Denmark and accounts for 30 ECTS points. The thesis a mandatory part of obtaining the degree ”Master
of Science in Biomedical Engineering.”
The thesis project is the foundation for the report and has been conducted in collaboration with
Rigshostitalet Glostrup and Stanford University.
The project includes signal processing tools for pre-processing of biomedical audio signals used in sleep
studies. Advanced machine learning algorithms in the form of state of the art deep neural networks are
trained and used to process audio into labels of speech and non-speech. The speciﬁc challenges of voice
activity detection for audio in sleep studies are uncovered and methods that attempt to handle these
challenges are presented.
i
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Analysis of sleep diseases and sleep stages currently requires overnight PSG recordings and trained per-
sonal to manually score the PSG. Much research revolves around automation of this process, where audio
is a promising modality, but the research area lacks a publicly available audio dataset.
In this project deep neural networks were used for automatic speech removal on stethoscope micro-
phones attached to sternum and throat a patient during PSG. The stethoscope microphones were specially
designed for sleep studies, called STETSNORE. The trained network were compared to the state of the
art unsupervised voice activity algorithm rVAD, since no other research has been done on this audio
modality.
The best performing network had an overall f1-score 0.45 and accuracy of 0.954, however with big
variance between diﬀerent patients. rVAD had an overall f1-score of 0.09 and accuracy of 0.932.
The results of the experiments showed that the used methods was successful to some degree of ﬁnding
speech in the recordings, but more work is needed. Speciﬁc challenges for audio in sleep research were
uncovered, where especially two challenges were eminent: Noises that could be confounded with speech
such as snoring and high-energy, long duration noise, and very low amplitude speech from other persons
in the room.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Resum´e
Analyse assøvnsygdomme og søvnstadier kræverlige nu at PSG optagelser skal laves natten over samt at
særligt trænet personel skal score PSG-optagelsen.
En del forskning omkredser at automatisere denne
process, hvor lyd har været særligt lovende, men ingen lyddatabase er desværre oﬀentligt tilgængeligt.
I dette projekt vil dybe neurale netværk blive brugt til automatisk fjernelse af tale i stetoskopmikro-
foner p˚asat sternum og halsen af patienter under PSG -optagelser. Disse mikrofoner var specielt designede
til søvnstudier og blev navngivet STETSNORE. Det trænede neurale blev sammenlignet med en state of
the art usuperviseret algoritme rVAD, da ingen anden forskning var lavet p˚a denne modalitet.
Det best performende netværk havde en samlet f1-score p˚a 0.45 og en accuracy p˚a 0.954, dog med
stor varians alt efter forsøgsperson. rVAD en samlet f1-score p˚a 0.09 og en accuracy p˚a 0.932.
Resultaterne af eksperimenterne viste at den anvendte metode var succesfuld til en vis udstrækning,
men mere arbejde er nødvendigt. Udfordringer speciﬁkke for lydoptagelser i søvnforsknin blev afdækket.
Særlig to udfordringer var vigtige: støj som kunne blive forvekslet med tale, s˚asom snorken og høj-
amplitude, lang varighed støj, samt lav-amplitude tale fra andre personer i rummet.
iii

3
Residual, gated dilated building blocks of the network. The input contained C layers which
underwent dilated convolutions and the output had 2C layers, which were split in two for
the activation and the gate before element-wise multiplication, and then outputting C
layers for the next layer. The dimension were kept constant with appropriate zero padding. 10
4
Full network architecture. The amount of convolutional layers, the sequence of dilation
and number of ﬁlters were all dynamic variables, which could be set for each experiment.
12
5
An example of the dilated convolution. (a) is the 1-dilated convolution and has a receptive
ﬁeld of 3x3. (b) is the 2-dilated convolution and has a receptive ﬁeld of 7x7. (c) is the
4-dilated convolution and has a receptive ﬁeld of 15x15. Adopted from[3]

==PAGE:15==
Introduction
Motivation
The diagnosis of sleep diseases and exploration of sleep stages has classically been, and still is, dependent
on performing a full polysomnography (PSG) as the gold standard. This procedure however has several
drawbacks: it is unpleasant, expensive and very labor intensive. The patient has to come into a sleep
clinic and sleep overnight while heavily equipped with sensors. Staﬀhave to be present overnight for
the PSG recording, and afterwards specially trained technicians have to manually go through the entire
recording to score the events.
One alternative for the PSG that is currently researched is the use of sound. This is a very information
rich signal, relatively cheap to record and easier to setup. Sound has already proven promising for diag-
nosing obstructive sleep apnea (OSA)[5,6,7] and for automatic sleep stage classiﬁcation[8,9,10]. However,
while larger PSG datasets such as Sleep Heart Health[11] and MESA[12] are available, no such database
for high quality sound with concurrent PSGs exists. This means that current research on sound for sleep
research cannot be reproduced or compared with each other, as each group has their own data.
This group has made an eﬀort to produce such a dataset by recording high quality audio concurrently
with PSGs at the Stanford University Sleep Clinic. Two microphones encased in a specially designed
”stethoscope” encasement were used, one placed on the throat and the other on the sternum. Thirty-ﬁve
recordings has currently been performed and has been dubbed STETSNORE, with more studies to come.
In order to make STETSNORE available to other research groups it has to be de-identiﬁed. This
means that any speech present in the recordings have to be removed, as it might contain personal health
information (PHI), where both the spoken words and the voice in of itself can be considered PHI.
This study will hence focus on developing a novel, fully automatic voice activity detector (VAD) based
on state of the art deep neural networks, speciﬁcally for removing speech from audio recorded during
sleep, and with STETSNORE as it’s main focus. A speciﬁc VAD for speech in sleep audio recordings
is important develop, since it would be desirable to have many hundreds or thousands of whole night
recordings available in the future. With each of them containing about 8-9 hours of audio, this quickly
becomes infeasible to do manually, and hence an automatic algorithm is needed.
The speech annotations were originally made on a another low-quality microphone in the room, which
was not synchronized with the STETSNORE microphones. Further, the time diﬀerence also drifts in two
1
==PAGE:16==
PROBLEM STATEMENT
INTRODUCTION
ways between them. This study will hence focus on preparing the STETSNORE microphones to be used
with the annotations and build a data pipeline to train and test a deep neural network on STETSNORE.
Since the amount of speech in STETSNORE was scarce, other databases were included to augment the
amount of available data for training of the neural network.
Problem Statement
The purpose of this study is develop a novel, fully automatic VAD speciﬁcally for speech in audio record-
ings in sleep studies, with aim of creating an fully automatic pipeline for de-identiﬁcation of these record-
ings.
To achieve this, the problem broken down into solvable sub aims. The following sub aims will outline
these problems:
 Prepare the STETSNORE dataset for analysis and use with the current speech annotations from
the in-room audio.
 Develop an understanding of relevant neural network architectures, how to implement these, and
relevant signal processing methods to pre-process the audio.
 Outline the state of art for voice activity detection methods through a literature study.
 Implement data pipeline necessary for training and testing a neural network.
 Establish what speciﬁc challenges of STETSNORE exists for the development of a fully automatic
VAD in this domain.
To achieve a fully automatic VAD algorithm speciﬁc for audio recordings in sleep studies the following
modules must be implemented:
 State of the art neural network for voice activity detection
 Experiments to properly determine the drift between in-room audio and STETSNORE, and a
pipeline to compensate for this.
 Data pipeline to convert the annotations of STETSNORE into an appropriate format for testing
the performance of a neural network.
 Data pipeline to process additional data from other databases into a appropriate format for training
a neural network.

THESIS OUTLINE
INTRODUCTION
Thesis Outline
This section outlines the structure of the thesis.
Theory Will describe the noise produced from a sleep and wake person along with common approaches
for voice activity detection. This leads into a state of the art literature study. The remainder of this
chapter will describe the necessary theory to build and train the chosen network architecture, along with
necessary signal processing for pre-processing the audio for the neural network. The rVAD algorithm is
also described here.
Methods Will describe the main dataset used, STETSNORE, including the process of aligning this
to the speech annotations provided, which were made on another microphone in the room. An overview
of the STETSNORE data is given with details on the amount of speech and data issues. Other audio
databases were included to augment the available data, which is presented here. The pre-processing of the
data into mel spectrograms is described and how they are presented to network. Lastly, the experimental
setups are outlined.
Results Describe the results of in all 6 experiments which aims at training a neural network to ﬁnd
speech in STETSNORE, as well as provide a baseline comparison with another pre-implemented voice
activity algorithm, rVAD.
Discussion Will discuss the results and bring about insight in potential problems and lay the foun-
dations for future work.
Conclusion Will conclude whether the aims of this project was reached and present ideas for future
work.

==PAGE:18==
Theory
This chapter will concern the fundamental theory of the experiments performed in this project. First
the fundamentals of producing sounds from the human throat is presented, where speech, snoring and
breathing were of most relevance for this project. This is followed by diﬀerent approaches in research to
voice activity detection (VAD), which leads into a presentation of state of the art within VAD.
Since no research have been done on testing the performance of VAD on a modality such as STET-
SNORE, the ﬁrst two papers presented concern the state of the art in general of VADs, and the last paper
presented concerns the state of the art of unsupervised VAD, which was developed to work cross-domain
and hence should in theory also generalize to STETSNORE.
The remainder of the chapter will describe the theory necessary to train a neural network based VAD
from audio. This includes conversion of audio into mel spectrograms and neural network architecture,
gradient descent and theory to speciﬁc to the chosen neural network architecture. Finally, the theory of
rVAD is presented.
Speech, snorring and breathing sounds
Both speech and singing is produced in the vocal tract by a vibratory excitation of this hollow anatomic
system, which collectively can be characterized as an acoustic box that is terminated at one end by the
glottis and the other end by the lips and nares. The primary sound generator within this acoustic box
is the vocal cords, which are operated by the air pressure produced by the muscles of the thorax and
abdomen. Adduction of the vocal chords causes them to narrow and vibrate, which produces a funda-
mental frequency and harmonics that deﬁnes the pitch of the voice. The supralaryngeal upper airways
(pharynx, nasal and oral cavities) functions as a resonating system that ﬁlters the sound produced by
the vocal cords, shaping it into a persons particular voice. The result is a concentration of acoustic
energy around a particular frequency, which is called a ”formant”. Three formants (F1-F3) are produced
simultaneously, where each formant corresponds to a resonance level in the vocal tract, deﬁning the
unique audible content that characterize diﬀerent individuals. The ﬁnal component of intelligible speech
is produced by the ”articulators” which is the set of movable structures (lips, tongue, soft palate and
jaw), that makes subtle changes in the frequency response of the resonator. The produced speech has
4
==PAGE:19==
VOICE ACITIVITY DETECTION
THEORY
three basic components: ”voiced sounds”, produced by the vocal cords, fricative and plosive (”unvoiced
sounds”), which are produced by laryngeal and supralaryngeal part of the vocal tract. The unvoiced
sounds are necessary to produce e.g. consonant sounds.[13]
Similar to speech, snoring sounds are also generated in the vocal tract[14], and acoustical analysis of
snoring and vocal sounds demonstrates that fundamental frequencies and harmonics (pitch) can occur in
both phenomena.[15] Formant features can be demonstrated as well in snoring sounds.[16] However, speech
and snoring sounds also have many dissimilarities. Snoring is produced by vibrations of the pharyngeal
structures, instead of the vocal chords. Endoscopic examinations have shown that during snoring, noise
is produced by ﬂutter of the soft palate together with other structures, such as tonsils, tongue base and
epiglottis. Further, the pressure is mainly directed interiorly, i.e. during inspirations and there is no
articulation.[17]
Prior research indicate that speech and voiced snoring can have quite overlapping frequency spectra,
but can be distinguished using higher order statistics, which also takes the phase of the signals into
account.[18] The authors show that the estimated transfer functions for speech and snoring are clearly
visually diﬀerent, but does not estimate the accuracy by applying it on a test set. Some research groups
have even applied methods for voice activity directly on snoring sounds to detect sleep apnea.[19]
Voice acitivity detection
Automatic speech recognition and speech synthesis has been as research topic for close to a century[20,21].
Much advancement has been made since the early 19th century, and many models exists. One subarea
of the speech recognition is voice activity detection (VAD), which only focuses on discriminating speech
from non-speech. This method helps speech recognition systems as a pre-processing step[22,23] and is
used in home systems such as Alexa, which continuously monitor if anything is said to it, in order to save
downstream processing costs[24].
Voice activity detection has mostly been based on the same features as speech recognition, as this
is implicitly the same task they are trying to solve, but without the decoding step speech recognition
has which maps sound to text essentially[25]. Voice activity detection is a binary classiﬁcation problem
where many features has been suggested through the years. Some of the more classic includes energy,
zero-crossing rate, hamonicity[26], entropy[27] and mel-ﬁlter banks[28]. Newer approaches has focused
mainly on mel-frequency ceptral coeﬃcients (MFCC) and power-normalized coeﬃcients (PNCC)[29,30],
and a posteriori signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) weighted energy distance[23,31,32]
The choices of classiﬁers for VAD have been heavily inspired by those of speech recognition, where
Gaussian mixture models have been very popular for both supervised and unsupervised VAD[25,33,34,35].
Other choices of classiﬁers also include decision trees[36], i-vectors[29,37], support vector machines[38], and
as a more recent advancement, deep neural networks[39,40,41].

==PAGE:20==
STATE OF THE ART STUDIES
THEORY
State of the art studies
SwishNet: A Fast Convolutional Neural Network for Speech,
Music and Noise Classiﬁcation and Segmentation
Authors: S. Hussain, A. Haque (2018)
Purpose: Produce a fast and light weight convolutional neural network for speech, music and noise
classiﬁcation/segmentation.
Methods: The authors build an 1D convolutional neural network which used frame wise MFCCs as the
input to the network. The network was trained and test on two corpora: MUSAN and GTZAN. The
MUSAN corpus consists of 109 hours of audio within music, noise and speech in 12 diﬀent languages, all
in 16 kHz WAV format. The GTZAN corpus contains 60 music ﬁles and 60 speech ﬁles. Each tracks is
30 seconds long and all ﬁles are in 22050Hz WAV format. The data were divided randomly in 65%, 10%
and 25% (training, validation and test sets, respectively).
For classiﬁcation, the data were windows sizes of eithr 0.5s, 1.0s or 2.0s with 50% overlap, while the
training data were 25ms with 15ms overlap, and 20 frames were concatenated at a time. Further, the
authors also tried using distillation from another pretrained network.
Results: The best performance SwishNet with distillation reached 99.84% classiﬁcation accuracy on
2.0s windows (classes were balanced), and 98.46% on framewise segmentation. MobileNet could perfom
marginally better, but SwishNet is about 7% the size of MobileNet.
Temporal modeling using dilated convolution and gating for
voice-activity-detection
Authors: S. Y. Chang et al. (2018)
Purpose: Beat the state of the art LSTM network at the time for VAD, while demonstrate the eﬀec-
tiveness of stateless CNN for temporal data.
Methods: The authors design a CNN very similar to WaveNet[42], which is made for speech synthesis,
but have not been tested on VAD. The most important components of the network are the use of dilated
convolutions, gated activations and residual connections. the data used were around 18,000 hours of
training data consisting of around 6.5 million English utterances, where noise was artiﬁcially added for
training. The noise was sampled from YouTube and daily life environments and added to the clean speech
to yield a SNR ranging between 0 to 30 dB. An LSTM was used as a baseline, where both networks had
about the same amount of parameters (400k).
The input features consisted of 40 log-mel ﬁlterbank energies, computed for a 25 ms window with 10
ms strides. A total of 270 left context frames (i.e. causal ﬁltering) was used for the input. The output
6
==PAGE:21==
STATE OF THE ART STUDIES
THEORY
was the framewise posterior of ”speech” or ”non-speech”, and varying thresholds was used to produce
the trade-oﬀbetween false positives and false negatives.
Results: The framewise posteriors were thresholded such that the false rejection rate of speech was ﬁxed
a 1%. This yielded a false accept rate of 5.67%, which was 14% above the current state of the art LSTM
network. Further, the researchers also included a graph of the trade-oﬀbetween false rejections and false
acceptances.
rVAD: An unsupervised segment-based robust voice activity de-
tection method
Authors: Z. Tan et al. (2019)
Purpose: The authors wanted to create a robust, unsupervised VAD that would work in both noisy and
non-noisy environments, since state of the art supervised methods usually do not generalize well to both
situations. Further, most VAD methods assume speech is present somewhere in each presented example,
which is rarely the case in real life.
Methods: The researcher deploys a two-stage model: In the ﬁrst segment high-energy segments were
detected and if they did not contain pitch, they were set to zero, and if they did, they were sent the
second pass. In the second pass, the signal was denoised with speech enhancement methods and speech
was detected using speech statistics. In the end a posteriori SNR weighted energy diﬀerence was applied
ﬁnd the boundaries of the speech segments. An alternative to pitch, spectral ﬂatness, was also presented
which speed up the algorithm about tenfold. The algorithm contained only one tuneable parameter.
To test their algorithm, the authors used the Aurora-2 database which contains clean speech mixed
with diﬀerent sources of noise, such as subway, babble, cars, among others. The speech had SNR values
ranging across 20, 15, 10, 5, 0 and -5 dB and 10 existing algorithms were compared with th proposed
method on this data.
Results: The authors reported the frame error (FER), which is equal to: 1 −accuracy. The FER for
their proposed algorithm was 6.90% for clean speech and 11.26% on average for the all the noisy speech
segments. This was a better performance than any of the other ten tested algorithms. The authors
further tried to change the tuneable parameter to examine the robustness of the algorithm, which showed
that the FER did not change much in a reasonable small interval around the optimal parameter value.

Mel Filterbanks
The Mel scale was designed to imitate the sensitivity of the human hearing by simulating frequency
response of the cochlea. Humans are much better at discerning small changes in pitch at low frequencies
than they are at high frequencies, which the Mel scale closely simulate.[43]
The formula for converting frequency to the Mel scale and vice versa is given by
M(f) = 1125 ln(1 + f/700)
(1)
M −1(m) = 700(exp(m/1125) −1)
Where M(f) is the Mel scale as a function of frequency.
To convert to Mel frequency ﬁlterbanks, it is ﬁrst necessary to take the discrete Fourier transform of
the signal
S(k) =

1 ≤k ≤K
Where s(n) is the samples signal, h(n) is a windowing function, typically chosen be the Hamming Window,
and K is the number points in the FFT, often chosen to be 512. However, since the discrete Fourier
transform assumes stability of the signal and speech is a highly dynamic signal, the signal is divided into
shorter, overlapping frames, where the signal can be assumed to be close to stable. The most common
choice for these windows are 25 ms with 10 ms overlaps.[44]
We then take the power spectral estimate of the speech frame si(n)

Neural network architecture
Feed forward neural networks
Feed forward neural networks, also called fully connected networks (FCN) are some of the most classic
types of neural networks.
This architecture was classically used for approximating the distribution,
which could classify the problem trained on or do regression of continuous outputs. Nowadays, these
networks are mainly as the ﬁnal discriminator after e.g.
convolutional layers, which in turn act as
features extractors. The same was the case for the architecture used in this project, at the very end of
the network, see Figure 4. An diagram of the feed forward neural network can be seen in Figure 2.
Figure 2: Illustration of the basic feed forward neural network. Illustration is adopted from.[2]
The fully connected layers works by propagating information forward trough each layer, where each
connection is a trainable weight (and possibly bias) and each neural sums up all these inputs and puts
the through an activation function, before passing this number to all the neurons it is connected two.

Residual connections and gates
The core of the network architecture was building blocks of gated unites with dilated CNNs and residual
connections. Residual connections are one of the recent year’s breakthroughs for deep neural networks,
where networks such as ResNet[46], DenseNet[47] and WaveNet[42]. The residual connections alleviates the
problem with vanishing gradients and propagates information better trough the network, hence allowing
to make the network deeper, which gives a clear advantage over a little older networks such as AlexNet[48]
and VGG[49]. A diagram of the gated, residual units which was the building blocks of the network can
be seen in Figure 3.

dimension were kept constant with appropriate zero padding.

convolutions that outputs 2C channels, which were then split into an activation stack (actual signal) and

CNNs and dilated convolutions
One of the more recent innovations of neural networks is dilated convolutions[3]. Dilated convolutions
were originally invented to increase the performance in semantic tasks which often needs a big receptive
ﬁeld for CNNs, but where this would result in have more parameters than what is practical or even
possible. Dilated convolution are the reason for success of state of the art performance of networks, such
as WaveNet[42], which need a very big context to produce good synthetic speech.
The dilated convolutions works by taking a normal convolutional kernel and intersperse it with a cer-
tain amount of zeros, hence dilating the size of the kernel without increasing the amount of trainable
parameters. The normal convolution can be written as
(F ∗k)(p) =
X
s+t=p
F(s)k(t)
Where F(s) is the convolutional operation in a point and k(t) is the ﬁlter itself. The dilated convolution
is then given by
(F ∗l k) (p) =
X
s+lt=p
F(s)k(t)
Where l is the convolutional factor and l = 1 will yield a normal convolutional operation.
An illustration of the dilated kernel can be seen in Figure 5. As it can be seen, when the dilated
convolutions are made in succession, the receptive ﬁeld increased exponentially, but without increasing
the number of parameters.
Figure 5: An example of the dilated convolution. (a) is the 1-dilated convolution and has a receptive ﬁeld
of 3x3. (b) is the 2-dilated convolution and has a receptive ﬁeld of 7x7. (c) is the 4-dilated convolution
and has a receptive ﬁeld of 15x15. Adopted from[3]
When looking at Figure 5, it might not be immediately clear why the receptive ﬁeld in e.g. (b) is 7x7
and 5x5, since the everything outside is the red dots are zeros.

Training of neural networks
The training of neural networks are carried out by iteratively moving closer to a local minimum using
gradient descent. The iterative solution is necessary as neural networks are highly non-linear structures
and hence no open form solution exist for an optimal solutions.
To train a network we are interested in ﬁnding an optimal set of weights that minimizes a given loss
function
w∗= arg min
w
E[L(f(x, w))]
Where L is the loss function, w is the weights for the networks and f(x, w)) is the network itself. For
each step of the gradient descent we then step one step closer so a local minimum
w(t) = w(t−1) + dw
The way we ﬁnd the dw is by diﬀerentiating the expected value of the loss functions and do a ﬁrst order
Taylor expansion to linearize the problem
E (w + dw) ≈E(w) + ∇E (w) = E (w) + dwT g
Where g is the learning rate. This is repeated until convergence.
16
==PAGE:31==
RVAD ALGORITHM
THEORY
Since diﬀerentiating into deeper layers involves diﬀerentiating the previous layers again, an eﬀective
way to reuse this calculations exist which dramatically speeds up the gradient descent procedure. The
method is called back propagation and uses the chain rule of diﬀerentiation.
Regularization
Overﬁtting is a very common problem in training of neural networks, where the network become too
sensitive to certain inputs and which will not be useful beyond the training data. To combat this, several
regularization methods have been developed to combat this.
Dropout:[50] This methods randomly zeros the input to next layer with a probability of p while training
the network. This essentially makes ”smaller networks” within the network that have seen diﬀerent parts
of the training data.
Batch normalization:[51] The method is normalizing the output of each layer before passing to the
non-linearity. This helps to alleviate the problem with vanishing gradients and also reduces the internal
covariance shift which arises due to diﬀerences of samples in each batch. Batch normalization allows for
faster convergence due to higher learning rates being possible, act as regularizer and makes the model
less sensitive to good initialization.
Weight decay:[52] Also known as L2 regularization add a penalty term to the loss function which
includes a fraction of the weights. This prevents the network from being too ﬂexible and hence generate
more general solutions rather than ones tightly ﬁtted to the training data. More data means a lower
weight decay and will usually mean a need for at bigger value.
Early stopping:[53] Early stopping is a very simple but eﬀective way of preventing over ﬁtting, and
have been eﬀective for neural networks despite it works less well in other applications. This works by
simply stopping training when no improvement is seen anymore on the validation set.
These regularization methods helps to ensure that the network will not overﬁt to the training data
instead of learning more general representation of the data.
rVAD algorithm
The rVAD algorithm is presented in the paper: rVAD - An unsupervised segment-based robust voice
activity detection method.[31] It is a very recent multi-step model which build upon previous research
from the group on a a posteriori SNR weighted energy diﬀerence measure.[23]
17
==PAGE:32==
RVAD ALGORITHM
THEORY
rVAD consists of the following steps: A ﬁrst pass denoising, which consists of high-energy segment
detection, pitch based noise-segment classiﬁcation, and then setting high-energy noise segments to zero.
In the second pass denoising, the algorithm will look for extended pitch segment detection, and lastly we
have the actual VAD.
In the noisy speech signal, the noise was assumes to be additive
x(n) = s(n) + v(n)
(2)
Where x(n) is the noisy signal s(n) is the speech signal and v(n) is the additive noise. The signal x(n) is
ﬁltered with a ﬁrst order high-pass ﬁlter with a cutoﬀfrequency of 60 Hz to remove any DC component
and low frequency.
The signal is then partitioned into frames of 25 ms with a frame shift of 10 ms, without a pre-emphasis
and windowing.
The next step is to calculate the a posteriori SNR weighted energy diﬀerence between two consecutive
steps
d(m) =
q
|e(m) −e(m −1)| ∗max (SNRpost(m), 0)
(3)
Where e(m) the energy of the mth frame of noisy speech x(n), m is the frame index, and SNRpost(m) is
a posteriori SNR which is calculated as the logarithmic ratio of e(m) to the estimated energy of the mth
frame of noise signal v(n)
SNRpost(m) = 10 ∗log10
e(m)
˜ev(m)
(4)
These noisy signal x(n) is then divided into super-segments of 200 frames (about 2s) as x(p) = s(p) +
v(p), p = 1, . . . , P, where P is the number of super-segments in an utterance. For each super segment the
noise energy ev(p) is calculated as the 10% lowest ranked energy the segment and thereafter noise energy
as calculated as a smoothed version with a forgetting factor of 0.9
ev(p) = 0.9 ∗ev(p −1) + 0.1 ∗ev(p)
(5)
The next step is second pass denoising.
The part is a spectral subtraction method based on speech
enhancement.
bλv(m, k) = Bmin ∗min{P(m, k), P(m −1, k), . . . , P(m −l, k)}
(6)
18
==PAGE:33==
RVAD ALGORITHM
THEORY
where k the frequency bin index, Bmin(m, k) is factor for compensating for the bias, m is the frame
index, P(m, k) is the recursively smoothed periodogram, and l is the ﬁnite window length for searching
the minimum, and bλv(m, k) is the denoised signal.
The next step is to ﬁnd sections with extended pitch. It is a fundamental assumption of the algorithm
that areas with speech will contain several frames with pitch. Areas with pitch found in the previous
steps are then extended from both sides with 60 frames (0.6s) and passed on to the VAD part.
a posteriori SNR weighted energy diﬀerence is now calculated on these segments and are classiﬁed as
speech if the they exceed a given threshold θV AD on a smoothed frame ¯d′(m)
θvad = β ∗1
L
L
X
j=1
¯d′(j)
(7)
Where L is the number of frames in all which contains pitch in the extended pitch segment. The default
set value for β is 0.4.
Post-processing is then applied, where pitch frames longer than 33 to left and 44 frames to the right
is classiﬁed as non-speech, as it is assumed the the pitched frames will be close in speech. These numbers
are empirically determined by the group based on the data they have tested on.
19
==PAGE:34==
Methods
This section will outline methods used to prepare the data for training of the neural network. The ﬁrst
part presents the data used: STETSNORE and the databases used to augment the available data.
For STETSNORE, it is described how the data was collected prior to this study and how the data was
prepared for analysis. A signiﬁcant amount of time was necessary to align the data to the speech labels,
which included manually going through each recording and creating an experimental setup to estimate the
time drift between STETSNORE and the in-room audio, which had to be manually conﬁrmed afterwards.
After STETSNORE, the other sound corpora used in the study is described.
Following this, the pre-processing of the data into mel spectrograms is described, which includes pre-
emphasis ﬁltering and adding noise to LibriSpeech for later experiments. Finally, the experimental setups
are described in 6 sections.
Data
Stethoscope Microphone (STETSNORE)
Lyndia Wu, PhD, from the laboratory of Ada Poon in collaboration with the Stanford Sleep Program,
created a simple stethoscope microphone” device that included a microphone and a resonance casing
made of silicone (see Figure 9a). Two microphones were placed on each patient: one on the thyroid
cartilage and one the sternum of the patient.
20
==PAGE:35==
DATA
METHODS
(a)
(b)
Figure 9: (a)Silicone encased microphone used as ”stethoscope” (b) The Phillips DVT 4010 recorder used
for the microphones placed on the neck and sternum of the patient.
The recordings were sampled overnight at 44.1 kHz with the Phillips DVT 4010 Recorder using two
condenser microphones plugged to the recording device. Audio was collected in a sleep laboratory at
the Stanford Sleep Clinic in parallel with at complete PSG setup, including oro-nasal pressure canula,
EEG, EOG, EMG channels. The patients also had detailed clinical data (a web-based questionnaire called
the Alliance Sleep Questionnaire). The placement of the microphone on the neck can be seen in Figure 10.
Concurrently, a video camera and microphone also captures the sleep study. The in room audio was
sampled at 8 kHz and used to manually label each instance of speech in the audio recordings by tech-
nicians. The neck microphone and the in room audio was then later synchronized in order to make the
speech labels match the neck microphone also.
Figure 10: Placement of the microphone on the neck. The other was placed on the sternum.
21
==PAGE:36==
DATA
METHODS
Alignment of In-Room Microphone and STETSNORE
Besides the neck microphones described above, in room sound was also recorded, which was part of the
standard procedure in the PSG recordings. As described, the in room audio was used for the technicians
to label the sections with speech.
However, since the desired signal to work on was the STETSNORE microphones, several challenges
had to be solved. Firstly, the neck microphones did only have a timestamp which was given with one
minute resolution. This was too much to get an unambiguous and reliable result when cross correlation
was tried to automatically align the sound signal.
Secondly, it was discovered that the neck microphone did not have a stable sampling frequency and
hence drifted over time compared to the in room audio.
From a few experiments done by using a
metronome and the neck microphone, it was discovered that this delay was relatively consistently 1
sec/hour compared to the in room audio.
The third problem consisted in .wav ﬁles are naturally limited to 2GB of size, and hence the entire
recording of the night was comprised of 3-4 .wav ﬁles. Each time these ﬁles were saved, a new somewhat
random delay was introduced, about 2-3 seconds per ﬁle.
Manually ﬁnd the same
keyword in both in room
and neck audio
Compensate for internal
drift of 1 sec/hour by zero
padding
Compensate for inter-ﬁle
drift of 2 sec/ﬁle
Stitch and compensate
for the delay in Matlab
Do ﬁnal manual quality
check to see if the
alignment was succesful
Figure 11: Flowchart over the process of aligning the in-room audio annotations with the STETSNORE
microphones
Since these problems could not be reliably be solved by an automatic algorithm, it was necessary to do
the alignment by hand, which was done by me on 35 recordings. The procedure was to load both the
in room audio and the neck microphone into Audacity together and the ﬁnding a spoken key word that
was quite distinct, and then ﬁnd the same spoken key word in the other recording. The time diﬀerence
between these two could then be used to align the two recordings. The neck microphones were then
zero padded to account for the above mentioned delays. An example of the ﬁnal result can be seen in
Figure 12.
22
==PAGE:37==
OTHER AUDIO DATABASES
METHODS
Figure 12: Example of aligned signal. The upper pane is in room audio, and the two lower panes are the
two neck microphones. Here the neck microphones had been zero padded.
STETSNORE data overview
In all 35 studies were available at the time of this project. Of these, 6 stethoscope microphones were
not turned on during the recording, 3 recordings corrupted and 1 ﬁle was missing. An overview of the
STETSNORE recordings and the speech content can be seen in Table 1.
The annotators were instructed to annotate any speech, which meant that TV during some of the
night was annotated as speech. However, the annotators was listening to the in-room audio, were the
TV was very clear. But for the stethoscope microphones the TV audio was often not very clear and
would often no be meaningful to include as speech. In hindsight, TV should simply have been annotated
separately.
Other audio databases
LibriSpeech corpus
In order to augment the available speech data, the LibriSpeech corpus was utilized.[54] The corpus consists
of over 1000 hours of speech from almost 2500 diﬀerent speakers. All the speech was produced by each
individual narrating a book and sampled at 16 kHz. The spoken language was English.
In order to prepare the data for this project, only a subset of the total 1000 hours of speech, where an
equal amount of each person we taken and these bites were all stitched together. The original purpose of
the data set was speech recognition, so aligned transcripts were also included in the data set, but were
not used for this project. The distribution can be seen Table 2.

AudioSet
Since snoring was quite prevalent in the neck microphones, more snoring data for training was desired.
However, snoring databases with large amounts of high quality snoring was very scarce, and also just
ﬁnding snoring in general. Luckily, Google has collected time labels for YouTube videos, spanning 485
diﬀerent categories over 1,789,621 segments, amounting to a whopping 4,971hours of audio.[4] One of
these categories was snoring, which is the one used for this project.
In order to extract the data, a batch script was written that used the list given in AudioSet to scrape
the videos from YouTube and convert them to wav. This classes comprised 2,334 videos amounting to
about 6.5 hours of sound. However, since some of the videos were both labeled snoring and speech, the
resulting length was 4.5 hours in all. The audio was sampled at 22050 Hz. An example of the hierarchy
of some of the categories can be seen in Figure 13.
Figure 13: Example of the hierarchy of the categories in AudioSet. This is only a subsample of all the
485 groups. Adopted from.[4]
FSDnoisy18k corpus
The FSDnoisy18k corpus consists to 20 classes with 42.5 hours of sound spread out on 18,532 audio
clips.[55] The majority of sounds stems from the AudioSet, but with better labeling. Out of the classes,
4 were chosen: ”Slam”, ”Tearing”, Dishes, pots, and pans” and ”Squeak”, as these sound were the most
realistic to be similar to the background noises of the sleep laboratory, where the neck microphone sound
was recorded. All the classes of FSDnoisy18k can be seen in Table 3.
To prepare the data, each of the 4 above mentioned groups were stitched together to one .wav ﬁle
25
==PAGE:40==
PRE-PROCESSING OF AUDIO
METHODS
and the mels frequency bins were computed for them.

QUT-NOISE
The QUT-NOISE-TIMIT corpus is a database of noise speciﬁcally made for development of VAD algo-
rithms[56]. The database consist of 10 hours of background noises divided on 5 diﬀerent settings:
 Cafe: noise and bable from Cafes
 Home: Kitchen background noise and living room with speech and TV noises
 Street: Background noises from two diﬀerent streets with traﬃc noise and nature noises
 Car: Two diﬀerent recordings inside a car, driving with the window up and down
 Reverb: Two reverberant places, inside a indoor pool and an enclosed car park.
The data set has previously been used to test other VAD algorithms by mixing it with speech in diﬀerence
signal-to-noise ratios. For this study, the ’home’ noises of children playing and ’Cafe’ was excluded as it
technically still contained speech and could confuse the neural network during training, which brought
the total length to about 8 hours.
Pre-processing of Audio
Converting Audio Into Mel Spectrograms
All audio was converted in to 26 log mel frequency bins with a window size of 25 ms and an overlap of
10 ms (see theory for details).
The overnight recordings contained around 8-9 hours of sound each, with in all 3 diﬀerent microphone
sources of the same night, placed on the neck and sternum of the patient, and a microphone in the room.
26
==PAGE:41==
PRE-PROCESSING OF AUDIO
METHODS
Aside from the speech, the recordings contain several diﬀerent acoustic events, e.g. snoring, sounds from
the patients turning over during night, heart sounds, doors opening and closing, among others.
Figure 14 Shows an example of speech and of snoring from all 3 microphones, with a female patient
and female technician in the room. Looking ﬁrst at the speech of the neck and sternal microphone Fig-
ure 14a, it can be seen that the speech of the patient saying ”that’s good” had a much bigger amplitude
the technician in the room saying ”is that okay?” and ”alright”, which also makes good sense since these
microphones are much closer to the patient.
In the spectrograms, it can be seen that the speech from the patient could be clearly seen as spectral
bands at the same time as ”that’s good”, where the pitch changes over times, which is what makes up
intelligible speech. For the speech of the technician (”is that okay? and ”alright”), the spectral bands
were still visible, but not as clear as for the patient. This makes sense when looking at the amplitude of
the technician speech in the panes above. When looking at the inroom audio, the spectral bands looked
similar to the other two microphones, but the recording contained a lot more noise in general. This also
makes sense, since the quality of the inroom microphone was much lower.
The lower pane contains the 26 mel ﬁlter banks. As described in the theory sections, the log mel
ﬁlterbanks distribute the log of the power spectrum with higher resolution to the lower frequencies and
less to the higher frequencies.

As described, the speech was labeled by a technician and used to produce samples of either speech of
non-speech from the log mel spectrograms. An illustration of how the frames were produced can be seen
in Figure 16. The green boxes represent the annotations for speech. In the upper pane the red boxes
29
==PAGE:44==
PRE-PROCESSING OF AUDIO
METHODS
represent how the speech frames were build and in the lower pane the yellow boxes depicts how the
non-speech frames were build. The length of the frames could be varied, and for this project 2.7 seconds
was ﬁrst used, but reduced to 0.9 seconds later. Notice the that windows were overlapping, which was a
simple technique to augment the amount of speech samples from the STETSNORE microphones.
Figure 16: Illustration of how the audio was processed into windows. The green boxes represent the label
of speech. In the upper pane, the red boxes represents the windows inside the speech annotations (these
can vary in length). In the lower pane, the yellow boxes represent the windows outside the speech labels,
which become the non-speech class.
Pre-Emphasis Filtering
All audio was processed into log mel frequency bins by using the ”python speech features” library.[57]
The library had the option to use a pre-emphasis ﬁlter
yt = xt −αxt−1
(8)
Where α = 0.97 for these experiment. The pre-emphasis enhances the higher frequencies and suppresses
any DC signal to some degree, and has classically been used in speech recognition systems. An example
of the eﬀect of pre-emphasis ﬁltering can be seen in Figure 17. On the mel spectrogram without pre-
emphasis more energy can be seen in the lower frequency range compared to the pre-emphasis ﬁltered
mel spectrogram, as expected. Further, a band of constant energy could be seen in the bottom of the
mel spectrogram that was not ﬁltered, which most likely belonged to the DC component of the signal,
which was gone on the ﬁltered mel spectrogram. Hence, the ﬁlter performs as expected on the data.
30

Examples of Pre-Processed Data From the Other Databases
To explore whether classiﬁcation of the diﬀerent datasets would be feasible, the data was visually inspected
after being converted into log mel frequency ﬁlter bins. An example of 10 sec of recording from each
of the data sets can be seen in Figure 18. It can be seen that the amplitude of high energy noise from
QUT-NOISE was comparable to both the speech and snoring in raw audio, and with some segments of
lower energy noise being smaller in amplitude. The spectral content of the speech was more concentrated
into a pitch and harmonics, while the QUT-NOISE seems to be more disseminated in general, but still
with some concentrations of a certain frequency range. The snoring has a pitch to some degree, but not
always with as heavy harmonics.

Neural Network training
This section will describe the experimental setups for training the neural network of each experiment.
The neural network used for all experimental setups was the one described in the state of the art
study: ”Temporal modeling using dilated convolution and gating for voice-activity-detection”.[45] Since
no implementation existed of this network, it was coded from the bottom up in PyTorch.[58] As a sanity
check to see if the network was correctly coded, it was tested on the CIFAR-10 dataset,[59] which consists
of 32x32 color images of 10 diﬀerent classes, e.g. automobiles, birds and cats. This was considered a more
complex task than the study at hand, and if it could learn on this, it was a network capable of learning.
32
==PAGE:47==
NEURAL NETWORK TRAINING
METHODS
Network Setup
As described in the theory section, the main body of the network consisted of these dilated, gated
convolutional layers. The amount of convolutional layers and the dilation scheme was parameters which
could be chosen, as well as the number of ﬁlters in each convolutional layer.
The speciﬁc detailed network architecture used can be seen in Table 4. Notice that the ”. . . ” means
the next convolutional layers. Batch normalization and dropout was also added between each of the
layers after the input layer. The dilation scheme was that same as used in the paper of the network:[45]
1, 2, 4, 8, ..., and hence the number of convolutional layers was multiple of 4.

Experiment 1: STETSNORE alone
In this experiment, only a few annotations for STETSNORE were available.
The annotations were
originally made by typing in the labels manually by hand in an Excel sheet, which was not very precise.
At this time in the study, 6 studies had been relabeled with Audacity for more precise labels (and a less
cumbersome experience for the annotators). The available annotations were made for: MIC05, MIC06,
MIC07, MIC11 and MIC23. A simple experiment with MIC05, MIC06, MIC07, MIC11 as training data
and MIC11, MIC21 as test data was set up. An overview of the data can be seen in Table 5.
This experiment was made to get an idea of how well a neural network could be trained with the
STETSNORE data alone.

When data is unbalanced, one of the best approach is to balance the data during training of the
network.[60] The most commonly used methods is randomly downsampling the larger class or upsampling
the smaller class. Both of these approaches were tried in this experiment. The setup of the network can
be seen in Table 6.
Notice that the convolutions were done along the frequency axis. This was done to make the algorithm
more robust to changes in pitch of the speaker, e.g. between males and females.

Experiment 2: LibriSpeech and Audioset snoring
A challenge of STETSNORE was the low amount speech data and also relatively low amount of subjects.
To better these parameters, the LibriSpeech corpus was used which contained 1000 hours of clean speech
divided on
2500 persons. In order to have a high diversity of speech, a sample of equal length from
all 2500 persons were taken to yield a 10 hour recording ( 17 sec per person). These samples were then
stitched into one long .wav ﬁle and converted to a mel spectrogram. This method was used to make it
easier to vary the length of the window shown to network.
To have a speech-free non-speech class which could represent the non-speech sounds from the STET-
SNORE data, snore samples from the Audioset corpus was used (described in the chapter about data).
These samples contained both snoring sounds, ambient noises and silence in between the snoring. The
Audioset consisted of 10 sec audio of snoring from diﬀerent YouTube videos. These were also stitched
into one long .wav ﬁle of ∼5 hours and then converted into a mel spectrogram, again to ease to pro-
cess of varying the windows length of mel spectrogram that were presented to the network. 5 % of the
FSDNoisy18k corpus and equal amount of samples from LibriSpeech was used as a validation set.
To see if the learning had any carry over to the STETSNORE, the network was tested one of the
studies (MIC05) as this had the most speech and was well labeled. The network was only tested on one
study as this time, since not many annotations were available at this point in the study and to keep the
time length of each run down to better be able to iterate.
The setup of the study used for the presented results can be seen in Table 7. Notice that more training
runs were made where the number of CNN layers, learning rate, dropout and weight decay was changed.
These results were not shown as it did not improve the performance.

Experiment 3: LibriSpeech and FSDNoisy18k
In this experiment Audioset was substituted with FSDNoisy18k. From the FSDNoisy18k corpus, the
4 classes that resembled the noises also found in STETSNORE were extracted, stitched together and
converted into a mel spectrogram. The used classes were: ’Slam’,’Tearing’, ’Squeak’ and ’Dishes and
pots and pans’, which collectively comprised about ∼4.5 hours.
The settings for the results presented in results chapter was the same as for experiment 2. Here the
window length, learning rate, number of convolutional layers were also varied but this did not change the
outcome, and are hence not presented in the results.
Experiment 4: LibriSpeech and QUT-NOISE
For this experiment, several things were changed in the setup. The following things were changed:
 Fewer persons from LibriSpeech were used, but with more data from each person. About 25 minutes
of speech for each person were divided into a 20 minutes training set and 2.5 minutes were used for
a test and validation set each.
 Longer periods of silence in between speech in LibriSpeech were removed to yield almost purely
speech in each frame.
 Previously each class had been standardized separately. Now all data for training were standardized
together, but each STETSNORE microphone was standardized by itself
 Audio ﬁles were no longer stitched into one long ﬁle, each audio clip was processed into 0.9 sec of
mel spectrogram
 A more realistic database, made for VAD development, were used: QUT-NOISE
 The 5 studies from STETSNORE with most speech was used to test on.
As stated, 5 studies from STETSNORE were included as a test set. These 5 studies were chosen since
they had the most speech, no annotated TV as speech and were annotated with the new method (in
Audacity, which was more accurate). An overview of these studies can be seen in Table 8

From LibriSpeech, 50 persons were used for training and 50 other persons for the validation. 2500 samples

if the setup was changed later.
As the non-speech class, the QUT-NOISE database was used, since this had more audio available,
and this database was speciﬁcally made to train VAD algorithms. QUT-NOISE contained ambient noise
recorded in homes, in a car and more (see the data section). QUT-NOISE was processed into log mel
spectrograms, and 10% of the QUT-NOISE were taken aside as a validation set. The setup of the network
can seen in Table 9.
Notice now that the length of the sample windows know were 0.9 seconds. This was changed due
much other research using shorter windows than the 2.7 seconds proposed by[45], where 0.9 seconds would
accommodate much speech, even if it was short.

Experiment 5: LibriSpeech, QUT-NOISE and Audioset
In this experiment, the setup was the as in experiment 4, but now with Audioset added again. This
time the audio was not stitched together before being pre-processed, but instead each 10 sec sample was
processed by itself.
A validation set with as many samples as for the QUT-NOISE and 50 LibriSpeech persons were made
(∼20% of Audioset snoring), and the rest was used for training. Since Audioset was about one third
of the two other datasets, it was upsampled during training. The snoring samples from Audioset were
added to the non-speech class.
In this experiment, the mean of each frame presented to the network was also explored, to see if the
network could simply use this as feature for distinguishing the two classes.
Except from this, the setup was the exact same as experiment 4.
Experiment 6: Adding Noise To LibriSpeech
Some of the speech in STETSNORE had a lower SNR, either because of people speaking less loudly or
the speech being mixed with other noise in the room. In an eﬀort to make the network more robust to
noisy speech, noise from QUT-NOISE was added to LibriSpeech to yield an average SNR of 3dB, or twice
the amount of signal to noise (see methods for details).
Two noise datasets from QUT-NOISE were used: ’CAR-WINDOWNB-1’, which was 45 minutes of
noise from driving in a car with the window down, and ’STREET-CITY-1’, which was 33 minutes of
background noise from a traﬃc intersection in a busy city. These two were chosen as they had more
constant background noise compared the e.g. the home noise recordings and the reverb noise recordings,
and only two were chosen to have less noise represented in the speech class, as the QUT-NOISE would
also be the non-speech class.
The power of a signal is given by
Px = 1

The noise was added by ﬁrst subtracting the mean from the current person from LibriSpeech and from
a piece of QUT-NOISE. The noise was then transformed into the desired average power:
Pdesired =

(10)
Where SNR is the desired SNR, Ps is the power of the speech signal and Pdesired is the appropriate average
power. The transformed noisy signal, n[transformed] and the speech signal s was then added together
smixed = s + n[transformed]
(11)
The mixed signal was then transformed into having the same power as the original speech signal from

Experiment 7: LibriSpeech and STETSNORE non-speech
Experiment 8: Comparison with rVAD
Since no other studies had been done on VAD performance on audio from sleep studies, the rVAD[31]
algorithm was tested as a baseline comparison. rVAD was chosen as baseline since this was the best
performing state of the art unsupervised VAD, which was developed to work cross-domain and was
extensively tested in the paper. Further, it comes with a complete Python implementation ready to use
and only have one threshold parameter to tune.
One drawback of rVAD was the long evaluation time of the STETSNORE. The evaluation of one
microphone recording of 8 hours length took about 1.5 hours, meaning about 15 hours for both throat
and sternum microphone for 5 STETSNORE subjects. Doing a grid search with any usable amount of
data points would quickly become infeasible. Instead, a subset of each of the sternum 5 microphones from
STETSNORE used in experiment 4-6 made for the grid search. Three minutes of speech, 3 minutes of
silence and higher amplitude noises, and 3 minutes snoring were manually picked out and the speech was
labeled. The sternum microphones were chosen as they had the most clear speech signal and this should
give the rVAD algorithm the best conditions for performing well. The whole process is also outlined in
Figure 23.
40
==PAGE:55==
EXPERIMENT 8: COMPARISON WITH RVAD
METHODS
Make subset of the 
STETSNORE sternum mics:
3 min speech
3 min noise
3 min snoring
Run rVAD on the subset data
for 50 linearly spaced
thresholds between 0.01 and 3
Convert both the rVAD labels
and manual speech labels into
labels of 0.9 second resolution
Run rVAD with the optimal
threshold on the STETSNORE
sternum microhpones
Convert both the rVAD labels
and manual speech labels into
labels of 0.9 second resolution
Evaluate the performance of
rVAD
Figure 23: Flowchart of how the grid search for the optimal threshold was performed and how the rVAD
was tested on STETSNORE afterwards.
To make the best possible comparison to experiment 4-6, the rVAD algorithm was run on the above
mentioned subsamples, and afterwards each recording was divided into 0.9 second segments. If anything
was labeled as speech within each 0.9 second window by the rVAD algorithm, this window was deemed
to be speech, and if not it was deemed non-speech. The same thing was done with the manual labels of
speech. Hence, the predictions were ”downsampled” to a resolution of 0.9 second.
For the grid search, 50 points linearly spaced between 0.01 and 3 was used (0.4 was the default
threshold). The threshold with the highest f1-score would then be used to run the rVAD algorithm on
the sternum microphones from STETSNORE used in experiment 4-6, and these would also be evaluated
by converting the predictions into 0.9 second windows as described above.
41
==PAGE:56==
Results
In this section, the results of experiment 1-6 outlined in methods are presented. The ﬁrst two experiments
were from more early in the process where less data was available, and was tested on one STETSNORE
microphone with the most speech to make iterating the design faster, as training and testing still took
signiﬁcant time.
Experiment 2-6 was more detailed, as more data now was available and the setup was more robust now.
Five STETSNORE microphones were used for testing and their individual performance was presented.
Lastly, the performance of rVAD is presented on the same 5 STETSNORE microphones.
Experiment 1: STETSNORE alone
Since the STETSNORE data was highly unbalanced (about 0-4% speech), two diﬀerent approaches for
training of the network was tried: The ﬁrst approach was to upsample the smaller class (speech) and the
second approach was to randomly downsample the bigger class (non-speech) by randomly drawing the
same amount of non-speech samples as the number of speech samples.
For all runs, 4 patients with the improved annotations was used for training and 2 was used as the
test set. The results are described below.
Upsampling of the speech
In the setup the smaller class (speech) was upsampled for each epoch. The network setup can be seen in
Table 6. In the ﬁrst run mel frequency bins were not standardized. The results can be seen in Figure 24.
The network seemed to learn the training set during 2-3 epochs, but kept improving on the loss and
accuracy of the validation set for almost all 30 epochs of training. However, since about only a few % of
the data were speech, the accuracy were not very good. This could also be seen on the f1 score of the
validation set, which was close to zero and did not really improve during training. The reason for this
was that the network just became more sure that everything was non-speech, including the speech.
42
==PAGE:57==
EXPERIMENT 1: STETSNORE ALONE
RESULTS
Figure 24: Upsampling of the speech class. The training set (green line) was balanced while the validation
set was not (red line).
To see if the results were due to the scaling of the mel ﬁlter banks, a new run were made with same
setup as in Table 6, but now the mels of each of microphone were standardized (subtract mean and
divide with the standard deviation). The results can be seen in Figure 25. It can be seen that the
convergence was much faster for the validation set in this run, which indicated that the standardization
was helpful. However, the result was still the same with a very low f1 score and the network hence had
little generalization to the validation set. The reason validation loss was lower than the training loss after
the ﬁrst epoch, was that the loss was calculated as mean during the entire training epoch, while the test
set was calculated after network has learned for the epoch.
Figure 25: Upsampling of the speech class. The training set (green line) was balanced while the validation
set was not (red line).
Downsampling of non-speech
To see if the network would learn better if the non-speech class instead was downsampled, the network
was trained this way and and the same parameters as in Table 6 were used. The results can be seen in
Figure 26. The results resembled very much those from the upsampling experiment above. The training
and test loss and accuracy improved as before, but the validation f1 score remained close to zero during
training, and hence the network did no generalize well to the validation set. Changining the learning
rate, changing the amount of layers, adding weight decay and dropout did now change the outcome.
43
==PAGE:58==
EXPERIMENT 2: LIBRISPEECH AND AUDIOSET SNORING
RESULTS
Figure 26: Upsampling of the speech class. The training set (green line) was balanced while the validation
set was not (red line).
Further, it was tried to change the learning rate, but this did not have any eﬀect on the outcome.
Experiment 2: LibriSpeech and Audioset snoring
Since training on the STETSNORE data alone did not seem to yield any good results, another approach
was tried that used speech and snoring with some noise from other databases (see methods).
The results can be seen in Figure 27 below. The network seemed to learn something on the training
data for the ﬁrst 1 or 2 epochs, but then it did not learn anymore on neither the training or validation
data. The training data was not perfectly balanced, with 2/3 speech, while the validation was balanced.
Hence, the training accuracy was better than random guess, but the validation was worse. The loss
functions for both the training and validation set kept decreasing slightly during the 30 epochs, but this
did not aﬀect the accuracy. As the STETSNORE data was highly unbalanced, the accuracy was close
to random guess. The low f1-score conﬁrmed that the network did not seem to learn any meaningful
representations of the training data that carried over to STETSNORE.
Figure 27: LibriSpeech as the speech class and snoring from Audioset as the non-speech class. The green
lines were training, red were validation and the blue were the microphone studies.
44
==PAGE:59==
EXPERIMENT 3: LIBRISPEECH AND FSDNOISY18K
RESULTS
Experiment 3: LibriSpeech and FSDNoisy18k
To asses whether the poor results were due the snoring simply being a too diﬃcult problem to solve with
this setup, or if there was an issue with the setup in general, Audioset was substituted with FSDNoisy18k,
which should be a more simple problem.
As it can be seen, the network seemed to now learn on the training set, but this did not carry over
to the validation set nor the STETSNORE set. A small amount of learning happened on the validation
set from the initialization of the network, i.e. before the training, but almost nothing on the following
epochs. The loss function also did not decrease for the validation. For the STETSNORE dataset, no
learning seemed to happen either, and the loss function also increased during the training.
It was tried to use dropout of 0.2 and 0.5, use weight decay of 10−5 and 10−4, range learning rate
from 10−4 to 10−7. However, none of these changed the performance of the network for the better.
Collectively, this indicated that something more fundamental could be wrong with the training setup
and a new approach was needed.
Figure 28: FSDNoisy18k as the non-speech class and LibriSpeech as the speech class. The blue line were
the STETSNORE
Experiment 4: LibriSpeech and QUT-NOISE
Since the previous learning setup did not perform well, a new setup were made, trying to correct things
that could be possible problems.
This setup gave better results than the previous. The results can be seen in Figure 29. As it can be
seen, both the training and validation converges very close to an accuracy of 100%. This now shows that
the network now was able to learn meaningful structures in data that also works on the validation set.
The high accuracy was not too surprising, as the ambient noise from QUT-NOISE and the speech from
LibriSpeech was quite diﬀerent.
Another indication that the network actually learned meaningful patterns in the data, was that both
accuracy and the f1-score of the STETSNORE data increased during training this time. The f1-score was
about 0.3, where in the previous experiment it was less than 0.15 (see Figure 28 and Figure 27). This
45
==PAGE:60==
EXPERIMENT 4: LIBRISPEECH AND QUT-NOISE
RESULTS
was a small increase, but nonetheless an indication that the new setup worked better.
Figure 29: Results for training on 50 persons from LibriSpeech and QUT-NOISE, which is represented
with the green and red line for training og validation split, respectively. The blue lines are the 5 STET-
SNORE studies which was only tested on.
To asses whether the performance was diﬀerent on the two microphones from STETSNORE, accuracy,
f1-score, recall and precision was plotted for each microphone separately as a function of epochs. The
results can be seen in Figure 30. The accuracy for the two microphones were very similar, which makes
sense since the majority was still non-speech and would hence dominate.
The f1-score was in general higher for the sternum microphone (about 0.35-0.4) than for the throat
microphone (about 0.2). This makes sense since the speech often saturated in the throat microphones,
making it distorted, while it rarely did it in the sternum microphones. Also, recall and precision followed
the same trend as the f1-score, where recall could reach a little more than 0.8. Some of the speech was
very low in amplitude (outside speakers) or very reverberant (people speaking from far away), which
would be the hardest speech to catch with the algorithm.
46
==PAGE:61==

of the 5 microphones. These were also the studies with the most speech of the 5, which could be one
of the reasons for this result. MIC09 and MIC31 had good recall for the sternum microphone but low
precision, which resulted in the overall low f1score.

Changing learning rate
The learning rate often one of the most important hyper parameters for deep neural networks to tune. To
make a simple test, the network was run with a learning rate of 5 · 10−4 (factor 10 increase) and 5 · 10−6
(factor 10 decrease) to see if this would improve performance. Only the results from 5 · 10−4 is shown as
the conclusions for both runs were similar.
The results of this run can be seen in Figure 32.
Comparing with the learning rate of 5 · 10−5 in
the Figure 29, the results were very similar with respect to the f1-score of STETSNORE. The loss of
STETSNORE was more erratic for the higher learning rate (5 · 10−4), which could indicate that this
learning rate was too high.
Figure 32: Performance on all 5 throat and sternum microphones from STETSNORE together. Blue line
is the accuracy (imbalanced), yellow is f1-score, green is recall and red is precision
48
==PAGE:63==
EXPERIMENT 4: LIBRISPEECH AND QUT-NOISE
RESULTS
The results of the throat and sternal microphone can be seen in Figure 33. Comparing with the learning
rate used in Figure 30, the pattern with the sternum microphone performing better than the throat
microphone was the same (higher f1-score). The f1-score was similar for both experiments, but it seemed
like that the higher learning rate (5 · 10−4) gave a trade oﬀbetween higher recall for lower precision,
which made the model more unspeciﬁc to speech.
Hence, (5 · 10−5) was deemed to be the most appropriate learning rate.

EXPERIMENT 6: ADDING NOISE TO LIBRISPEECH
RESULTS
Examining the means of the samples
Since the network was able to learn to diﬀerentiate the two classes close to perfect, it could be speculated
that some simple diﬀerence existed between the datasets. The most obvious suspicion could be that the
network simply learned to distinguish the mean between the two classes, since this could potentially be
diﬀerent for the diﬀerent classes. To asses whether this was the case, the mean of all samples from all
the classes were calculated and a histogram of the distribution was made. The results can be seen in
Figure 38. From the histograms it was clear the distribution of the mean values of the samples were not
clearly separated, hence the network have had to learn something more complex than this.

Figure 38: Histogram of the mean of each sample (0.9 sec mel spectrogram) from the 3 datasets: Lib-
riSpeech, QUT-NOISE and Audioset.
Experiment 6: Adding noise to LibriSpeech
Some of the speech in STETSNORE had a lower SNR, either because of people speaking less loudly or
the speech being mixed with other noise in room. As an eﬀort to make the network more robust to this,
noise from QUT-NOISE were added to LibriSpeech to yield a SNR of 3dB, or twice the amount of signal
to noise (see methods for details).
The result of this run can be seen in Figure 39. The network was again able to learn the diﬀerence
between the two classes with almost 100% accuracy in the training and validaiton set, even thought it
took slightly longer for this run. The f1-score for STETSNORE in this run was comparable to that of the
one without noise in Figure 29, but the performance ﬂuctuated less, indicating the patterns learn from
the data was closer to a more stable solution.
53
==PAGE:68==
EXPERIMENT 6: ADDING NOISE TO LIBRISPEECH
RESULTS
Figure 39: Results for training on 50 persons from LibriSpeech with 3dB SNR noise added and QUT-
NOISE, which is represented with the green and red line for training og validation split, respectively.
The blue lines are the 5 STETSNORE studies which was only tested on.
The results from the throat and sternum microphone can be seen in Figure 40. When comparing this with
no noise added in Figure 30, the f1-scores were very similar for both the throat and sternum microphone.
The recall seemed in general slightly lower for the current run with added noise, while the precision
seemed slightly higher, which explains the similar f1-scores for the run with and without noise.

Experiment 7: Comparison with rVAD
In this experiment the rVAD algorithm was tested as a baseline comparison to the trained neural networks
in the other experiments.
55
==PAGE:70==
EXPERIMENT 7: COMPARISON WITH RVAD
RESULTS
The rVAD algorithm was primary unsupervised, but had one threshold parameter which had to be
tuned. As described in the methods section, a subset of the STETSNORE data used in experiments 4-6
was used to do a grid search for the optimal threshold. The results can be seen in Figure 42. As it can
be seen, two maxima existed in this range: 0.25 and 1.9.
0.0
0.5
1.0
1.5
2.0
2.5
3.0
Thresshold
0.26
0.28
0.30
0.32
0.34
0.36
0.38
0.40
f1-score
Thresshold search rVAD
Figure 42: Threshold parameter search for rVAD used on STETSNORE. In all 50 points were linearly
spaced between 0.01 and 3. The used metric was f1-score.
In order to asses which of these maxima was most appropriate to use, the labels made on the subset data
was inspected, to see if a clear qualitative diﬀerence existed. An example of this for MIC05 for 0.25 can
be seen in Figure 43 and 1.90 can be seen in Figure 44, for both the same 65 seconds was shown.
Looking at the results for a threshold of 0.25, it could be seen that rVAD algorithm in general seem
over sensitive. For speech it did mark all the speech, but also seemed to mark almost everything else
as speech. For the noise section, it also labeled almost the entire recording. Notice that the heart beat
sounds e.g. from 30 seconds to 45 seconds was also marked as speech. For the snoring, most of the
snoring was also mistaken for speech together with some other noise in that recording.
Then looking at the threshold of 1.90, the rVAD predictions overall seemed much less oversensitive.
For the the speech part, it still predicted the noise at the 50 second mark as speech, and in general there
was not too many false positives. However, much of the speech was also missed. The patients own voice
seemed in general to be well recognized, e.g. around the 7 second and 20 second mark. However, lower
amplitude speech (from other persons in the room) was in general not found. For the noise, the heartbeat
sounds were now not marked speech and the noise before the 25 second mark was also not falsely marked
as speech. However, the high amplitude noise around the 25, 50 and 55 seconds mark was still falsely
marked as speech. For the snoring pane, only the snoring with highest amplitude was falsely labeled as
speech, and the remainder of that pane was not.
56
==PAGE:71==
EXPERIMENT 7: COMPARISON WITH RVAD
RESULTS
Collectively the threshold of 1.9 seemed to be most speciﬁc to labeling speech and had a more reasonable
sensitivity to noises in the signal. Hence this values was used to test rVAD on the 5 STETSNORE
sternum microphones.
