==PAGE:73==
Discussion
The aim of this project was to train a neural network based VAD speciﬁc to the domain of audio record-
ings in sleep studies. Up until now state of the art approaches has been examined and the most promising
papers has been presented. The theory necessary to build and train such a neural network was described
along with the theory of how to pre-process the audio for the training of the neural network. Much
eﬀort have gone into aligning and processing the STETSNORE study to have the speech annotations ﬁt
STETSNORE. A state of the art neural network for VAD has been coded and trained on several diﬀerent
data setups, and tested on the most suitable subset of the STETSNORE microphones.
The ﬁrst experiment (Experiment 1: STETSNORE alone) was an early exploratory experiment to see
that a neural network would learn on the STETSNORE data available at that time. Two approaches
was used: random downsampling and upsampling of the larger non-speech class. As seen in thhe results,
the network seemed to mainly learn the non-speech class, which gave a high accuracy in the validation
set, but since 98.7 % of the validation set was non-speech, this is not too surprising. The f1-score was
close to 0 in both cases, which indicated that the network did not learn any meaningful representations
of speech in the data. This was however not too surprising given the low amount of speech in this data
setup, despite the eﬀort to balance it during training.
The next experiment (Experiment 2: LibriSpeech and Audioset snoring) tried to address the problem
of the low amounts of speech in STETSNORE by augmenting the data with the LibriSpeech corpus.
To further asses if the snoring was the main challenge of network the snoring from the Audioset corpus
was used as the non-speech class. The network reached a training accuracy of 78%, while the validation
accuracy kept being 38% and the f1-score of STETSNORE was about 0.05. The most things were most
likely problems with the setup. The ﬁrst was that LibriSpeech had silence in between sentences, which
could confuse the network when this was presented as a speech class. The second problem was most
likely how the datasets was normalized. Since the microphones were under very diﬀerent conditions and
diﬀerent microphones were used for LibriSpeech and Audioset, the mel spectrograms for each database
were standardized separately, which most likely introduced an unnatural bias in the data. Also, using
2500 persons and only 17 seconds for each might also have been too diverse without enough examples for
each person.
Experiment 3 (LibriSpeech and FSDNoisy18k) was an eﬀort to examine whether the poor perfor-
59
==PAGE:74==
DISCUSSION
mance due to LibriSpeech vs. Audioset was simply a too diﬃcult problem for the network. The training
accuracy was higher for this experiment with 89%, but the validation only reached 60% and stayed
there. The f1-score for STETSNORE was about 0.13 and the accuracy was low, indicating that the net-
work mainly guessed the samples from STETSNORE as being speech. This setup improved the overall
performance slightly, but the setup still need more adjustments, which leads to the following experiments.
In the following experiment (Experiment 4: LibriSpeech and QUT-NOISE), the data setup was changed
on several parameters, among them the ones mentioned above. The silence in between the utterances
in LibriSpeech was removed to yield frames with mainly speech in them. Fewer persons were used but
more data from each person was used. Further, exactly 2500 samples for training and 250 for test was
used for each person, to make balancing easier. The mean of and standard deviation for both classes
we calculated and used to standardize LibriSpeech and QUT-NOISE together, rather than separately.
Further, more annotations arrived for the STETSNORE and 5 microphone studies were included.
As it could be seen from the results, the network was now able to distinguish LibriSpeech and QUT-
NOISE with close to 100% accuracy. This was not too surprising under the better conditions, as Lib-
riSpeech and QUT-NOISE was quite diﬀerent in the the mel spectrograms. The f1-score also increased for
STETSNORE reaching about 0.35. In these experiments the individual performance of the microphones
were also assessed. The experiment indicated that speech in general was harder to classify in the throat
microphone than in the sternum one. This would make sense as the speech of the patient often saturated
in the throat microphone, as it can be seen in Figure 15. The individual microphones did also have quite
diﬀerent performance. In general MIC05 and MIC34 faired better. These also had more speech that the
other, which might have helped. Further, these were the ones I had labeled during the relabeling process,
and the labels was more tight around the speech. MIC34 was labeled directly on the STETSNORE data
instead on the in-room, which could also be an explanation for the better performance, simply because
less silence was labeled as speech. It was also tried to increase and decrease the learning rate in this
experiment, but this did not beneﬁt the performance of the network.
For Experiement 5: LibriSpeech, QUT-NOISE and Audioset, the snoring from Audioset was used
again for the non-speech class. As the results indicated, this actually seemed to have an overall detrimental
eﬀect judging from the f1-score. Overall the precision seemed to increase slightly, which would make sense
as maybe less snoring would be labeled as speech. However, this was as the expense of recall, which did fall
quite a bit. Most likely the snoring in Audioset was too diﬀerent from what was found in STETSNORE
or the microphone quality was simply too poor. Since the network was able to distinguish the LibriSpeech
from QUT-NOISE and Audioset, a sanity check was made. The mean of all the standardized samples
from each class (that is, the 0.9 mel spectrograms presented to the network) and the distribution of the
means where plotted. The distributions were very much on top of each other, meaning that the network
could not simply distinguish the classes by the amount of energy in a frame. However, since the network
could learn the diﬀerence so quickly a relatively simple diﬀerence most likely exists between the classes,
60
==PAGE:75==
DISCUSSION
that the network quickly exploits.
In an eﬀort to prevent the network from ﬁnding simple shortcuts in the data, noise was added to
LibriSpeech in Experiment 6: Adding noise to LibriSpeech. The noise was added in a SNR of 3dB, which
was clearly visible on the mel spectrograms, but most of the pitch and formants was still preserved. This
time it took the network slighty longer to reach close to 100% accuracy on the training and validation set,
but it was still reached. The overall performance on STETSNORE did not improve for this experiment.
The recall was slightly lower and precision was slightly higher. The higher precision could make sense,
as the network would be more robust distinguishing noise and speech. The lower recall might be because
the network became less reactive to pitch and formants in the mel spectrograms, as they were now less
pronounced in the training data.
As a baseline comparison, the rVAD algorithm was tested on the same 5 STETSNORE microphones
as in experiment 4-6. The rVAD algorithm has been developed over many years by the research team
and was tested extensively in the paper on several speech and noise databases and under diﬀerent SNRs.
However, despite the brilliant performance in the paper, the rVAD did not perform well STETSNORE.
The overall f1-score was 0.09 on the sternum microphone, as opposed to about 0.35 for the best run of
the network.
This indicates that some of noise content was either so fundamentally diﬀerent from was is usually
encountered in a VAD problem that the developers of rVAD could not take this into account, or some of
the noise content was similar enough with speech to be confounded with it. Looking at Figure 15, the
snoring clearly had pitch and formants, event though these were much less erratic than in speech. As
described in theory, very little research has concentrated on the distinguishing snoring and speech. Two
papers from the same ﬁrst author[61,18] focus on estimating the total airway response (TAR) from audio
recordings, where the paper from 2007 compares the TAR of snoring and speech. The paper states that
snoring can be ”voiced” or ”unvoiced”, where voiced means that it contains pitch, just as speech. The
paper concludes that higher order spectra (HOS), which are based on third order cumulants, are necessary
for distinguishing voiced snoring and speech, instead of an amplitude spectrum. However, the authors
only shows examples of this, but does not provide any quantitative performance measure for their method.
Collectively, the STETSNORE had two major challenges as VAD a problem. The ﬁrst was the acoustic
content that could be confounded with speech. For both rVAD and the neural network this seemed to
be high energy noise over longer periods, e.g. when the patient was moving around in bed, and snoring,
specially snoring with a high amplitude. The sounds from the heart beat did also seem to confuse both
algorithms, but to a lesser degree. The second problem was the speech from other people in the room,
which was very low amplitude and could also be reverberant. Reverberant speech is usually treated as it
own challenge in VAD and speech recognition literature, as this makes the problem harder.
61
==PAGE:76==
Conclusion
The overall goal of this study was to produce a novel, fully automatic algorithm to remove speech from
audio in sleep studies to hence de-identify them. This goals was described as sub goals, which will now
be assessed.
The STETSNORE data was successfully prepared and aligned with the available annotations. An
understanding of relevant neural network architectures and signal processing methods to pre-process the
audio was developed, along with an outline of the state of the art through a literature review. Lastly, the
data pipeline for testing and training the network was implemented, along with a state of the art neural
network.
The trained network was successful to some degree on ﬁnding speech, with recall between 0.63-0.83
for the sternum microphone, with a precision between 0.11-0.66. Both are wished to be higher with
more future experiments, but as of now the network could act as a pre-labeling algorithm before manual
inspection of the recording, to save time compared to having to through the entire recording. However,
the future goal is still to make the algorithm fully automatic.
Through the presented experiments on STETSNORE, the speciﬁc challenges of audio in sleep re-
search was established. Two major challenges were uncovered. The ﬁrst was the acoustic content of
STETSNORE that could be confounded with speech, such as snoring and high amplitude and long dura-
tion noise, e.g. when the patient moves around in bed. The second being the speech from other persons
in the room, which had very low amplitude and could be reverberant. These topics are hence target for
future research.
Future work
As mentioned, more future work is needed to make the algorithm fully automatic in removing speech
from sleep audio recordings.
The labels were originally made on the in-room audio, and despite the good alignment, the labels will
still be somewhat noisy due to the stochastic nature of the time delay in the recorder for STETSNORE.
Hence, for future work a new relabeling is most likely needed, with more detailed classes, such as patient
speech, in-room speech, TV, and maybe others. This will help to break the problem down into smaller
sub-problems that can then be solved more focused.
62
==PAGE:77==
FUTURE WORK
CONCLUSION
As STETSNORE did not oﬀer much speech, the future recording sessions could turn on the micro-
phone in the beginning of the sleep study, which will yield more than an hour of speech. Alternatively,
volunteers could read from a book with the microphones attached.
Alternatively, a better correspondence between the acoustic content of LibriSpeech and STETSNORE
could be established through new experiments using advanced signal processing, which could lead to
development of a ﬁltering technique for making LibriSpeech more domain speciﬁc to STETSNORE,
specially for the low amplitude speech.
Other decomposition methods could be tried, such as higher order spectra described earlier. Other
decomposition methods could include e.g. wavelet transforms or correlations methods.

’ ’ ’
APPROCH WITH ADDING NOISE FROM QUT−NOISE TO LIBRI

’ ’ ’
Function
f o r
adding
noise
with a desired SNR
’ ’ ’
def addNoiseSNR (x , n ,SNR) :
# x :
input
s i g n a l
# n :
noise
s i g n a l
# SNR:
desired SNR ( not
in
d e c i b e l s )
# x wil be retransformed back to
the same average power in
the end to not
increa
# Transfom : Y = a*X => a = sqrt ( s 2 / s 1 )
# remove mean
x = x−np . mean(x)
n = n−np . mean(n)
138
==PAGE:153==
A. PYTHON SOURCE CODE
# c a l c u l a t e
target
power
P x = np . var (x)
target P n = (1/SNR)* P x
# transform
noise
to
t h i s
power
n = n*np . sqrt ( target P n /np . var (n ))
# add the
noise
x = x + n
# transform back to
the same power as
o r i g i n a l l y
y = x*np . sqrt ( P x/np . var (x ))
return y

function
[ a c t i v i t y i d x ] = findActivity (aud , fs , wnd expansion )
% takes
audio and
f s
in
% gives
back

vector

l o c a t i o n s
% wnd expansion : how many seconds
to

the window
% RMS envolope
s i g n a l
10 ms windows
env aud = sqrt (movmean( aud .ˆ2 , round (0.1* f s ) ) ) ;

b a seline
noise

f o r
i = 1: f l o o r ( length ( aud )/( f s *5))

end
i f
any ( isnan ( wnd vec ))
error ( ’NaNs ’ )
end
% get
0.05
p e r c e n t i l e
lowest

% smooth

broader

a c t i v i t y i d x = a c t i v i t y i d x > 0;
end

function
timeDiff = clock2sec ( refTime , time )
% Converts 24 hour time to
seconds ,
assuming
that
recordings do not span
% more than 2 consecutive
days .
% Time format has to be : HH:mm: ss . S
refDay = ’1985 −01 −01 ’;
% Check
i f
we have passed
midnight
( 0 0 : 0 0 : 0 0 )
i f
str2double ( refTime (1:2)) <= str2double ( time ( 1 : 2 ) )
timeDay = ’1985 −01 −01 ’;
e l s e
timeDay = ’1985 −01 −02 ’;
end
tRef = datetime ( [ refDay , ’
’ , refTime ] , ’ InputFormat ’ , ’ yyyy−MM−dd HH:mm: ss . S ’ ) ;
t = datetime ( [ timeDay , ’
’ , time ] , ’ InputFormat ’ , ’ yyyy−MM−dd HH:mm: ss . S ’ ) ;
timeDiff = seconds ( t−tRef ) ;
end
